TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
DTU CHEMICAL AND BIOCHEMICAL ENGINEERING
MASTER THESIS
INVESTIGATION OF METHODS FOR ESTIMATING INTERFACIAL TENSIONS
Authored by
Christos Valiantzas (s202624)
Supervisor:
Georgios Kontogeorgis
June 24th, 2022
==PAGE:2==
Summary
During the last years, a very challenging topic in chemical society regards the surface tension cal-
culation of various solid surfaces. Many equations have been developed through the years; never-
theless, none is accurate enough. Moreover, all the developed equations have numerous limitations
that hinder their wide use. From the classical developed equations, van Oss/Good seems the most
satisfactory, despite its drawback with the high basic surface tension components. However, in the
last decade, an innovative approach has been proposed by professor Costas Panayiotou in a series
of papers which combine quantum mechanics and statistical thermodynamics. In that approach
the surface tension components are calculated from molecular descriptors called partial solvation
parameters (PSPs). In the Panayiotou equation, an asymmetrical geometric mean rule is used for
the hydrogen-bonding component, similarly to the van Oss/Good approach. The purpose of this
master thesis is to provide a systematic evaluation of numerous polymer surfaces by applying the
Panayiotou equation and comparing the calculated surface tensions with the early-developed equa-
tions.
Surface tensions are one of the most fundamental properties that characterize a surface. They can
be used in plenty of applications, such as cleaning, glues, paints, and the development of waterproof
surfaces. Contact angle measurements of many different solids have been reported in the literature
and used for calculations.
Panayiotou method showed remarkable agreement with the van Oss/Good method, while slight di-
vergences were observed from Owens/Wendt and Hansen/Beerbower methods. The study focused
on the calculation of polymer’s surface free energy. Finally, a contact angle prediction was real-
ized for all the methods. Panayiotou method showed the most outstanding results among the four
methods.

1
Introduction
1.1
Personal motivation
The interfacial tensions are an exciting topic in surface chemistry. Personally, there are many rea-
sons that I decided to focus on it. Firstly, it is a topic with numerous applications in industry, while
wetting and adhesion phenomena have many employment prospects in companies. Furthermore,
the dominant scientific controversy that has been continued for decades is an incentive that arouses
my interest and motivates me to deal with surface tensions. For more than forty years, a perfect
equation has not been developed. The scrutinization of the new method of Panayiotou is a chance
for an innovative investigation of a topic that has concerned researchers for years. The possibility
of having a breakthrough in an extravagantly disputed issue is stimulating.
1.2
Study objectives
This study’s purpose is to analyze the various ways that the pioneering method of Panayiotou can
be used. The Panayiotou method can provide accurate results of surface tensions by using one
or two experimental data from contact angles. Many different solvent combinations and liquid
pairs have been investigated to evaluate the most efficient approach. Another target of the study
is to compare the Panayiotou method with the existing equations and distinguish the differences
between them. The comparison is realized in two steps. Firstly, the surface tension of various
polymers is estimated and compared, while secondly, a contact angle prediction is performed. The
literature reported contact angles were evaluated experimentally, and their importance is crucial for
the assessment of the best method.
1.3
Analysis of solid surfaces
1.3.1
Surface tensions
One of the fundamental thermodynamic properties that can describe a surface is surface tension or
surface free energy. Surface tensions can be defined as the work required for increasing the area of
a surface, isothermally. The interpretation of many phenomena, like wetting, adhesion, spreading,
and lubrication, can be explained by surface tensions. The most significant wetting applications
are umbrellas and raincoats, while adhesion applications are divided into two major categories;
desirable and undesirable. The first encompasses glues, paints, coating, adhesives, and fibre-matrix
composites, while the second one has applications in cleaning and biofouling. When the surface of
a solid is higher than the surface of a liquid, the liquid wets that surface [2].
Forces of liquid molecules are distributed unequally in the bulk and the surface. That happens
because molecules tend to bond with other molecules around. In bulk, they have plenty of choices
to be conjoined; however, molecules can only bond to each other in the interface. That creates a
"layer" in the surface that can bear a specific small weight on it. That can explain how some insects
can walk on the water surface, as seen in Figure 1 [2].
1
==PAGE:13==
Figure 1: Insect staying in water surface due to the high surface tension of water [1].
Surface tensions are proportional to temperature changes, are expressed with the symbol "γ", and
their universal units are mN/m. The term interface describes liquid-liquid and liquid-solid surfaces,
while the term surface refers to a liquid-vapour and solid-vapour surface. Two fully miscible liquids
have zero interfacial tension. Hydrogen-bonding compounds have higher surface tensions than the
rest of the compounds. Generally, polymers have low surface tension values, while glass, ceramics,
and metals have high [2].
1.3.2
Work of adhesion and contact angle
Two important equations strongly linked with interfaces are the Dupre equation of work of adhesion
(1.1), which is defined as the required work for the separation of two surfaces, and Young’s equation
of contact angle (1.2) [2, 11, 12].
Wadh = γs +γl −γsl
(1.1)
cosθ = γs −γsl
γl
(1.2)
The validity of equation (1.2) depends on the so-called spreading pressure, which represents the
vapour adsorption on a solid surface [13]. Spreading pressure is defined as the difference between
the solid surface tension and the solid surface tension in the presence of air (πsv = γs−γsv). Young’s
equation requires spreading pressure to be equal to zero. Spreading pressure can be considered
negligible for low surface tension surfaces. Contamination of a surface also affects the contact
angle values, so high purity surfaces are required before measuring a contact angle. A contact
angle with a lower value than 90 o corresponds to partial wetting of the solid surface, while higher
than 90o, to partial non-wetting. Contact angles equal to 0 o and 180 o represent complete wetting
and non-wetting, respectively [2].
In the literature, contact angles of various liquids have been reported for many polymer surfaces.
The contact angle database of each polymer can be seen in Chapter 8.1.
2
==PAGE:14==
2
Classical Methods
In the last decades, many different equations have been developed to describe the solid-liquid in-
terfacial tensions. Surprisingly, despite the abundance of the existing equations, no equation can
perfectly describe the interfacial tensions, and all have their limitations.
2.1
Girifalco/Good
The Girifalco/Good equation was the first equation that provided a method for interfacial tension
estimation. It developed in early 1957 and took its name from professors Louis Girifalco and Robert
Good. The Girifalco/Good equation is called "direct" because it is not having equations, providing
surface tension components.
γsl = γl +γs −2·φ ·√γl ·γs
(2.1)
The parameter φ is a correction parameter, which is calculated from the molar volume of the solid
(Vs) and the molar volume of the liquid (Vl).
φ =
4·(VsVl)1/3
(V 1/3
s
+V 1/3
l
)2
(2.2)
The equation of interfacial tension can be combined with Young’s equation of contact angle to find
the surface tension of the solid and gives the following equation:
cosθ = −1+2·φ
rγs
γl
(2.3)
Equation (2.3) requires one experimental data from the contact angle to calculate the unknown solid
surface tension.
Girifalco/Good equation has limited practical use due to problems with underestimations or over-
estimations of the correction parameter φ [2].
2.2
Neumann equation-of-state
One of the most controversial surface tension equations is the equation-of-state, proposed by Neu-
mann, which is based on the modified Berthelot’s rule [14].
γsl = γl +γs −2√γl ·γs ·e−β(γl−γs)2
(2.4)
The constant β decided to have a universal value of 0.0001247 (m2mJ−1)2. After plenty of calcu-
lations, the authors observed that the slight differences in the values of β do not significantly affect
the calculated interfacial tension. Hence, the assumption of a fixed value was reasonable [14].
3
==PAGE:15==
Equation-of-state can be combined with Young-Equation and gives the following equation:
cosθ = −1+2
rγs
γl
e−β(γl−γs)2
(2.5)
Equation (2.5) has γs as the only unknown parameter, so one experimental data from the contact
angle is required to calculate the solid surface tension.
Despite some good results and numerous published articles, the equation of Neumann has been
criticized extensively for not accounting for strong chemical effects, such as hydrogen bonding [2].
2.3
Fowkes equation
The first developed surface component equation was "the Fowkes equation," which took its name
from professor Robert Fowkes, the inventor of this equation. This equation is a modification of
the "direct" Girifalco-Good equation. According to Fowkes, the surface tension of a component is
equal to the sum of each component’s forces [2].
γ = γd +γp +γi +...
(2.6)
The subscript "d" stands for dispersion forces, while "p" and "i", for polar and dipole forces, re-
spectively. The interfacial tension between two components is described in the equation:
γsl = γl +γs −2·
q
γd
l ·γds
(2.7)
When this equation is merged with the contact angle equation, the solid surface tension components
can be calculated from:
cosθ = −1+2
q
γds ·γd
l
γl
(2.8)
This equation requires one contact angle experimental data to calculate the unknown solid disper-
sion term.
The primary assumption of the Fowkes equation of interfacial tensions is that the cross-interaction
term across the interface is due to dispersion forces only [2].
The equation of Fowkes can perfectly describe systems with hydrocarbons, especially alkanes,
where only dispersion forces are present. Also, it has shown outstanding results for water-mercury
systems [2]. However, as referred to previously, every equation has its weaknesses. Fowkes equa-
tion has shown problems in describing systems like water-aromatics, water-amines, water-alcohols,
water-anilines, and in general hydrogen-bonding compounds [2].
4
==PAGE:16==
2.4
Owens/Wendt equation
The Owens/Wendt equation is an extension of the Fowkes equation. The surface tension of a
component is defined similarly with Fowkes, with the equation:
γ = γd +γspec
(2.9)
The term "spec" includes the polar and hydrogen-bonding interactions. The difference between the
two equations is an additional term added in the interfacial tension equation, where Owens/Wendt
also considers the specific interactions by adding a geometric mean rule in the interfacial tension
equation [2].
γsl = γl +γs −2·
q
γd
l ·γds −2·
q
γspec
l
·γspec
s
(2.10)
The combination of the Owens/Wendt equation with Young’s equation gives:
cosθ = −1+
2
q
γds ·γd
l +2
q
γspec
s
·γspec
l
γl
(2.11)
In equation (2.11), there are two unknown solid components; dispersion and specific interactions,
so it requires two experimental contact angle data [2].
Owens/Wendt equation has shown excellent results for polymer interfaces but still has its weak-
nesses in systems like acetone-water and ethanol-water, which predicts miscibility between these
solvents [2].
2.5
Hansen/Beerbower equation
Hansen/Beerbower is also an equation that is considered an extension of Fowkes. The difference
between Fowkes and Owens/Wendt is that it has separated the "spec" term into polar and hydrogen-
bonding terms. The subscript "d" stands for the weak dispersion London and the Debye induction
interactions, while "p" accounts for the Keesom dipole-dipole orientation interactions [6]. The
surface tension of a component is defined, similarly, to the previously mentioned equations, as the
sum of dispersion, with polar and hydrogen bonding forces [2].
γ = γd +γp +γh
(2.12)
The interfacial equation has assumed three geometrical mean rules for dispersion, polar, and hydrogen-
bonding interactions [2].
γsl = γl +γs −2·
q
γd
l ·γds −2·
q
γ p
l ·γ p

q
γh
l ·γhs
(2.13)

A difference between the equations is how the individual components γd, γp, and γh are calcu-
lated. These components are calculated via the Hansen solubility parameters (δd, δp, δh) from the
equations (2.14), 2.15, and 2.16 [2].
γd = 0.0715·V 1/3 ·δ 2

(2.14)
γ p = 0.0715·V 1/3 ·l ·δ 2

(2.15)
γh = 0.0715·V 1/3 ·l ·δ 2

(2.16)
where V is the molar volume in cm3mol−1 and "l" an adjustable parameter. Hansen solubility
parameters are expressed in (cal ·cm−3)0.5 [2].

for the estimation of the solid surface tension components as follows:

The existence of three solid surface tension components leads to the necessity of having three
contact angle data and solving a system of three equations with three unknowns.
Hansen/Beerbower’s equation of interfacial tension and the Owens/Wendt equation has been criti-
cized extensively due to the use of geometric mean rules for the asymmetric polar and hydrogen-
bonding interactions.
2.6
Van Oss/Good equation
In 1987, van Oss and Good developed a new approach for estimating interfacial tensions. The
novelty of this equation is the use of an acid-base term for hydrogen-bonding interactions. The
sum of this asymmetric term with the van der Waal forces (LW) gives the total surface tension [2].
γ = γLW +2
p
γ+ ·γ−
(2.18)
The "LW" term stands for the Lifshitz/van der Waal forces and encompasses the weak London
dispersion, the Debye dipole, and Keesom dipole-dipole interactions [6]. The symbol "+" is defined
as the acid forces and "-" as the base forces. The interfacial tension between two components can
be calculated from the equation (2.19) [2].
γsl = γl +γs −2

the following equation:

(2.20)
Similarly, with equation (2.17), there are three unknown solid surface tension components, which
means that this equation requires three experimental contact angle data and a system of three equa-
tions with three unknowns for their determination [2].
After numerous calculations, it was shown that equation (2.20) performs better when the defined
liquids are comprised of two polar liquids and one non-polar (or apolar). One of the polar liq-
uids is usually decided to be water. A non-polar liquid has defined as a liquid that contains only
London/van den Waal term, meaning that the acid-base terms are zero [2].
Van Oss/Good equation, assume that water compound has equal acid and base values at 20 oC
(γ+
water = γ−
water = 25.5mN/m). An innovation of this method is the possible occurrence of negative
values of interfacial tension, which indicates that the liquids are miscible. However, despite the
great anticipation for the results of this method, it also has its problems. The major limitation of
this method is the overestimation of the basic component of many liquids, and polymers [2, 15].
2.7
Discussion
Many equations describing the interfacial tensions have been developed through the decades, but
their individual emerging weaknesses have led to extensively criticizing and arguments about which
one should be used. The equations and their weaknesses are presented in Table 1.
Table 1: Weaknesses of the different methods for interfacial tension estimation.
Method
Weakness
Girifalco/Good
Over- and under-estimation of the correction parameter φ
Neumann (Equation-of-state)
Not accounting for strong chemical effects
(e.g hydrogen bonding)
Fowkes
Water-aromatics
Water-amines
Water-anilines
Water-alcohols
+ other hydrogen bonding compounds
Owens/Wendt
Water-ethanol
Water-acetone
Hansen/Beerbower
The geometric mean rule for asymmetric polar, and
hydrogen bonding interactions is not theoretically
correct.
Van Oss/Good
Overestimation of base component
The most promising of the equations mentioned above is the Van Oss/Good, where the published
results are satisfying, and it has broad applicability in describing systems such as polymers, paints,
7
==PAGE:19==
and proteins [2]. Many researchers investigated the reason for the high basic compounds of this
method. They concluded that the problem occurs from the equality of acid and basic components
of water, which is used as the neutral compound. Lee et al. [5, 15] proposed that the ratio between
the acid component of water and the basic part should be equal to 1.8. Della Volpe et al. [16]
proposed a ratio of 5.5-6.5, and some years later, a ratio of 4.35 [17]. However, these investigations
have not been used in practice, and the van Oss/Good defined ratio of 1.0 is still used in industry.
Owens/Wendt has shown satisfactory results for determining polymer surface tensions in the litera-
ture and is one of the most used equations in industry. Therefore, it will comprise the representative
equation for comparison with the Panayiotou method. Hansen/Beerbower method will also be com-
pared to the Panayiotou equation to investigate how the geometric mean rule of hydrogen-bonding
interactions affects the overall surface tension of the polymer. Finally, a comparison with the van
Oss/Good method will be performed to assess the similarities between the two approaches.
3
Panayiotou-PSP method
3.1
Surface tension equation
In 2012, professor Costas Panayiotou proposed an innovative method for calculating the surface
tension of the solids. In this approach, the total surface tension equals to the sum of the dispersion,
the polarizability, and the hydrogen-bonding part, with respective subscripts w, pz, and hb [6].
γ = γw +γpz +γhb
(3.1)
where the hydrogen-bonding component can be described similarly, to Van Oss/Good theory, as an
asymmetric square root component as follows:
γhb = 2·√γa ·γb
(3.2)
Thus, the interfacial tension between two components is defined in equation (3.3), while the com-
bination with Young’s equation can calculate the surface tension components from equation (3.4).
γsl = γl +γs −2(√γw,l ·γw,s +pγpz,l ·γpz,s +√γa,l ·γb,s +√γb,l ·γa,s)
(3.3)
(cosθ +1)·γl = 2(√γw,s ·γw,l +pγpz,s ·γpz,l +√γa,s ·γb,l +√γb,s ·γa,l)
(3.4)
A great difference with the Van Oss/Good equation (2.19), is the use of a separate term for dis-
persion and polarity terms. The summation of γw and γpz terms in equation (3.1) is equivalent to
the LW term of Van Oss/Good equation. However, the major difference between these equations
is how each surface tension component is calculated [2, 6]. Each part is calculated from molecu-
lar descriptors called partial solvation parameters (PSPs), which combine elements from quantum
mechanics with QSPR/LSER solvatochromic, and solubility parameter approaches [18].
8
==PAGE:20==
3.2
QSPR and LSER background
QSPR approach is used to predict and determine various critical thermodynamic properties. One
of the earliest QSPR techniques is the solubility parameter method, which uses three molecular de-
scriptors, called Hansen Solubility Parameters (HSP). The three HSPs represent dispersion, dipolar,
and hydrogen bonding interactions and are expressed with δd, δp, and δhb, respectively [18, 19].
Another widely used QSPR technique is the linear free energy relationship (LFER), or linear
solvation-energy relationship (LSER), where five molecular descriptors are used. These are the
McGowan volume, dipolarity/polarizability, excess molar refraction, hydrogen bond acidity, and
basicity are indicated with Vx, S, E, A, and B, respectively [8, 18, 20].
In COSMO-RS theory, structural or van den Waals atom properties have been optimized for nine
elements (H, C, N, O, F, S, Cl, Br, and I). COSMO-RS model calculates the system free-energy
of mixing and other fundamental thermodynamic quantities (when the molecular surface charge
distributions -COSMO files- are available). As a continuous distribution, the surface charge dis-
tribution has its statistical moments known as COSMOments. COSMOments are used to correlate
the LSER molecular descriptors and the Hansen Solubility Parameters [18].
3.3
Partial solvation parameters (PSPs)
Firstly, four partial solvation parameters were introduced; dispersion, dipolar, hydrogen bond acid-
ity, and hydrogen bond basicity. Later, dispersion and dipolar were replaced from three new PSPs,
which were the weak van den Waal non-polar interactions, the dipolarity/polarizability, and the ex-
cess molar refraction of the compound. LSER descriptors A and B were mapped onto the hydrogen
bond acidity and basicity free energy PSPs [18].
Two different schemes of PSPs were developed:
i) s-scheme
ii) σ scheme
These two schemes have two common things. Firstly, the hydrogen bond PSPs that are identical
in both two schemes, and secondly, water neutrality [18]. However, water is considered an almost
neutral compound, but in reality, it is a slightly acidic compound. To make it completely neutral,
the basic descriptor B should be slightly shifted by multiplying it with a factor of 1.084, so A=1.084
B [6, 20]. Then, m-SUM can be defined as m-SUM=A+0.1098B. So, now hydrogen-bonding PSPs
can be defined as:
σ2

The dispersion and polar PSPs of the s-scheme are equivalent to the Hansen dispersion and polar

equation:

This connection between the two schemes is beneficial because information between the set of the

The dispersion PSP (σw) reflects cavity effects, hydrophobicity, and dispersion, or weak non-polar
interactions. It also lays the ground for negative PSPs (since LSER descriptors E and S may have
negative values) [7, 18, 19]. Van der Waals energy of COSMO-RS satisfy all these conditions that
σw should reflect, so that this PSP can be defined as:
σw =

(3.10)
where Vmol is the molar volume of the component. The symbol "w" stands for dispersion and
weak van der Waal forces. The difference with the dispersion part of the Owens/Wendt equation,
indicated with "d", is that the "d" accounts for both dispersion and Debye induction forces [6].
The PSPs that map the LSER descriptors E and S are σE and σS. These can be combined into a
polarizability/refractivity PSP [18].
σpz = σE +σS
(3.11)
The polarizability PSP reflects dipolar interactions, and it can be obtained from the cohesive energy
density (ced) of the compound as seen in equation 3.12 [18, 19].
σ2

(3.12)
However, equation (3.12) is not valid for all the compounds, leading to the need to divide them into
two classes; homosolvated and heterosolvated. Homosolvated compounds, where equation (3.12)
is true, can self-associate. Heterosolvated can cross-associate, which means another compound is
needed to reveal its hydrogen-bonding capacity. Heterosolvated are not hydrogen-bonded in their
pure state [18].
The equation for the polar PSP of heterosolvated compounds is:

(3.13)
where σpz+ encompasses contributions that should be attributed to hydrogen-bonding interaction
[18]. The typical units of partial solvation parameters are MPa0.5 [6, 8, 18, 19].
3.4
Surface tension components
The van der Waal PSP (σw) represents an energy density, while the respective surface tension
component represents energy per unit area. COSMO area (Acosmo), and COSMO volume (Vcosmo),
have been considered to be representatives of the solvation area and volume, respectively [6, 8].
So, the London dispersive surface tension is defined as:
Vcosmo ·σ2
w = Acosmo ·γw = Ew ⇒
γw = Vcosmo
Acosmo
σ2
w
(3.14)
As seen in equation (3.14), the dispersion or van der Waal part, depends on the Vcosmo/Acosmo
ratio, and the dispersion partial solvation parameter σ2
w [6]. For the surface tension calculation of
polymers, a prevalent problem is the poor availability of the Vcosmo/Acosmo ratio. That can be solved
by calculating the ratio from the monomer of the respective polymer from the equation (3.15) ([6]).
(Vs
As
)
polymer
= (Vcosmo

)

(q

monomer
(3.15)
where q and r are the UNIQUAC/UNIFAC- type parameters, which provide information about the
geometry of the substance [6].
The equation for the free energy change for a hydrogen bond formation between acidic molecule i
and basic molecule j is defined as:
GH
ab,ij = −fσGa,iσGb,j
p
Vmol,i ·Vmol,j +g
(3.16)
where f=1.70 and g=2.50 kJ/mol [18]. Thus the hydrogen bonding surface tension of interacting
molecules i, and j are defined as:
γhb,ij = fσGa,iσGb,j

(3.17)
Equation 3.17 is equivalent to the asymmetric square root of the acid and basic component, as
defined in equation 3.2.
The polar or hydrogen-bonding groups are more likely to be oriented toward the bulk liquid phase
than the interface, in contrast to weak van den Waal interactions, that show no orientational char-
acter [6, 8].
11
==PAGE:23==
High surface densities may be counted as hydrogen-bonding sites when interacting with comple-
mentary hydrogen-bonding sites or polarity/Polarizability sites when interacting with non hydrogen-
bonding counterparts [8].
That gives the following equations for the polarizability surface tension components:
γpz
2·γa
=
σ2
pz
u·σGa ·σGhb
(3.18)
γpz
2·γb
=
σ2
pz
u·σGb ·σGhb
(3.19)
When the equations (3.18) and (3.19) are combined with equation (3.1), lead to the following
equation:
γpz =
γ −γw
1+ u(σhb·√σGa·σGb)
σ2pz
(3.20)
The term "pz" stands for both the Debye induction forces and the Keesom dipole-dipole orientation
interactions when, the indication "p" of the previously mentioned methods accounts only for the
Keesom interactions [6]. The factor "u" represents the peculiarities of the molecular structure
and the surface charge distribution that are not accounted for in partial solvation parameters. The
parameter "u" varies with hydrogen-bonding components, and a value of 2 corresponds to the van
Oss value for water 25.5 mN/m and is considered universal. Therefore, the parameter "u" in all
the previously mentioned equations will be deemed to be equal to 2. That will allow making a fair
comparison with van Oss/Good theory. [6].
4

ponents and the overall solid surface free energy. In the Owens/Wendt method, a system of two
equations with two unknowns is solved to calculate the polymer’s surface tension component. The
dispersion and specific terms of many solvents are summarized in Table 2.

Abbreviation letters are used for the solvents of Table 3. The abbreviation DIM corresponds to
diiodomethane, while W, F, Br, and P.O., correspond to water, formamide, α-Bromonaphthalene,
14
==PAGE:26==
and paraffin oil, respectively. For the most polymers, the liquid pair was diiodomethane and water.
Formamide was used instead of diiodomethane for PMMA, PVC and PVAc. Paraffin oil was used
only to calculate PVAc’s surface tension, while α-Bromonaphthalene was used for Teflon FEP.
Different liquid pairs lead to slightly different surface tensions. All the investigated polymers show
little deviations from literature reported tensions. The overall surface tensions of PMMA, PVC, and
PE reported by Owens et al. [23] were slightly higher than the calculated tensions. That happened
because Owens et al. [23] used his method with diiodomethane and water as a liquid pair instead
of formamide and water. The literature reported surface free energy of PP was calculated by Oss
et al. [3] and it is around 3 mN/m lower than the result calculated from Owens/Wendt equation.
Literature is utterly ambivalent about PS’s surface tension. Lee et al. [5] used the van Oss/Good
method and calculated PS’s free energy as equal to 42 mN/m, while Neumann et al. [14] used their
method to measure is equal to 28.90 mN/m. The calculated result with the Owens/Wendt method
is closer to the solution reported by Neumann et al. [14]. Zdziennicka et al. [34, 35] used the
van Oss/Good, and Owens/Wendt approaches to investigate the surface tension of Nylon-6. When
the liquid pair of Owens/Wendt was water and diiodomethane, the surface tension fluctuated from
42 to 45 mN/m. When formamide replaced water in the pair of solvents, the surface free energy
was reduced to 40.1 mN/m. Zdziennicka et al. [34, 35] were repeated the calculation with the
van Oss/Good method, and the overall tension fluctuated from 38 to 40 mN/m. Kwok et al. [27]
used Neumann’s equation-of-state method with many different liquids to prove that the surface free
energy of PBMA fluctuated from 28 to 30 mN/m. In that Chapter, Owens/Wendt’s calculations
showed a 4 mN/m higher PBMA tension. Lee et al. [5] used the van Oss/Good method to calculate
the surface tensions of PIB, FEP, and PTFE equal to 25.0, 15.7, and 19.6, respectively. These
literature values diverge approximately 2 mN/m from the calculated tensions with Owens/Wendt
method. Finally, Ozbay et al. [22] used Owens/Wendt and van Oss/Good methods to investigate
the surface free energy of PPS. They used many different pairs of liquids and triplets and used the
mean value as the overall surface tension of the polymer. The two method results fluctuated from
41 to 43 mN/m, which agrees with the calculated tension.
5
Van Oss/Good method calculations
Equation (2.20) requires three experimental data from contact angles. The polar liquid pair used
for most estimations was water and formamide. The liquid triplet was completed with the non-
polar diiodomethane. For PVAc surface tension calculation, the non-polar solvent was paraffin oil,
while for Teflon-FEP was α-Bromonaphthalene. For PS calculations, glycerol was used instead of
formamide due to higher accuracy in its contact angle value in the literature. The liquid surface
tension components and the contact angles are provided in Table 5.

17
==PAGE:29==
The selected solvent triplet is very important for the final result. High deviations can be obtained
when three polar liquids are used instead of two and one non-polar. For PIB, there were not three
available liquid contact angle data; however, it is considered a non-polar polymer in the literature,
so it is reasonable to assume that the acidic and basic terms are zero. Consequently, one non-polar
liquid, diiodomethane, is adequate for its overall surface tension calculation. Lee et al. [5] reported
the surface tension of PIB, equal to 25 mN/m, by using the van Oss method. The LW term using
diiodomethane as the non-polar liquid equals the total surface free energy and approximately 23
mN/m. Excellent agreement is observed for the calculated surface tension of PMMA, PVC, and
PE with the ones evaluated by Owens et al. [23]. Lee et al. [5] used the van Oss/Good method
to calculate the surface free energy of PS equal to 41.5 mN/m, which diverges less than 0.3 mN/m
from the calculated tension. Slight divergences are observed between the calculated surface tension
of PP and the value measured by Lee et al. [15]. Kwok et al. [27] used Neumann’s equation-of-
state to calculate the polymer’s surface free energy of PBMA. Their measured tension diverges
around 5 mN/m from the one calculated with the van Oss/Good method in that Chapter. Kwok et
al. [14, 37] were also used that approach for PS surface tension calculation. In that case, their result
was much lower than the calculated value of 41.30 mN/m. PEA shows high deviations between the
literature reported contact angle by Schwach et al. [32]. However, the contact angle of water used
in that polymer was very underestimated compared to the water contact angles reported by Sanchez
et al. [41], and Pastor et al. [33]. Nylon-6 is in great agreement with its literature reported value
by Zdziennicka et al. [34, 35], when they used the van Oss/Good approach. The surface tension of
PTFE has been calculated with three different approaches in the literature. Lee et al. [5] used the
van Oss/Good method, Kwok et al. [37], Neumann’s equation-of-state, and Owens et al. [23] used
the Owens/Wendt equation. Lee et al. [5], measured the surface tension equal to 19.6 mN/m, while
Kwok et al. [37] and Owens et al. [23] equal to 18.0 mN/m. The calculated surface tension agrees
more with the tension calculated by Owens et al. [23]. Kwok et al. [37], were also calculated the
surface free energy of PET to be 35.2 mN/m, while Owens et al. [23] to be 41.30 mN/m. The
calculated value of this Chapter agrees more with the tension calculated by Owens et al. [23].
PVDF’s literature surface tension, measured by Kwok et al. [37], is in good agreement with the
calculated one. Finally, slight divergences were observed between the evaluated surface tensions
of FEP and PPS and the tensions reported by Li et al. [38] and Ozbay et al. [22], respectively.
Van Oss/Good results for PMMA and PVC are in good agreement with Owens/Wendt method
calculations (Chapter 4) with 6.6 % and 1.7 % deviations, respectively, in the overall calculated
surface tensions. In contrast, for PS calculation, the deviation is 44 %. The low dispersion and
specific components in Owens/Wendt method have led to a low surface tension value for PS. In
contrast, the LW part in van Oss/Good theory (a feature that contains both dispersion and polar
interactions) is almost double. A possible explanation for this is that in the Owens method, polar
and hydrogen bonding interactions are combined in the same term, expressed with spec. If the polar
interaction is high and the hydrogen bonding interaction is minimal, the whole term automatically
gets a meager, creating this considerable deviation.
Another possible explanation may occur by looking at the critical surface tension of PS. As critical
is defined, the surface tension of the liquid that yields exactly zero contact angle [2]. Critical surface
tensions firstly, reported by Zisman et al. [42], in 1964. They are calculated from the Zisman plot,
where cosθ and γl are plotted. For PS, the reported value is 33 mN/m [2]. This value corresponds
to a liquid of 0 o contact angle. A characteristic of these parameters is that their value is very close
18
==PAGE:30==
to the solid surface tension [2]. However, for PS, it is not very helpful because its value is between
the calculated values of Owens/Wendt and van Oss/Good.
6
Hansen/Beerbower method calculations
The first step for calculating a polymer’s surface tension with the Hansen Beerbower method is to
estimate the liquid surface tension components. In order to achieve that, the total liquid surface
tension, the Hansen solubility parameters (HSPs), and the molar volume of the solvents should be
known. That will be used afterwards for the calculation of the adjustable parameter "l". The Hansen
solubility parameters and the molar volume of many different solvents are provided in Table 8.

The factor 0.0715 and the equation parameters’ units have been adjusted to have the surface ten-
sion components in mN/m. With "l" known, the dispersion, polar, and hydrogen-bonding terms
can be calculated from the equations (2.14), (2.15), and (2.16). At the same time, the individual
calculations for water can be seen in equations (6.4), (6.5), (6.6). The calculations are summarized
in Appendix A.3.

Three experimental data from contact angles are needed to calculate the polymer’s surface tension.
Hansen/Beerbower equation is then solved by a system of three equations with three unknowns.
20
==PAGE:32==
The sum of dispersion, polar, and hydrogen bonding terms will provide the overall surface free
energy of the polymer. Two or more different triplets have been tried for the polymers with adequate
available literature data. In most cases, the most accurate triplet was a triplet consisting of two
polar liquids and one non-polar liquid. Formamide and glycerol were the polar solvent pair for the
majority of the polymers, while α-Bromonaphthalene was the non-polar.
PMMA is the first polymer to be investigated. Van Oss et al. [3] have reported the contact angle of
formamide on PMMA’s surface equal to 55.5 o, while Owens et al. [23] have reported the angle of
water to be 80 o. The angles of glycerol and α-Bromonaphthalene were reported by Neumann et
al. [14] and Volpe et al. [39], to be 66.84 and 36.50 o, respectively. By substituting these contact
angles in Hansen/Beerbower equation, equations (6.9), (6.10), (6.10), (6.12) and can be obtained.

Due to adequate available literature data, two different triplets were tried. The first one consists of

literature reported tension by Owens et al. [23], which was equal to 40.20 mN/m. The same pro-

the calculation for polyisobutylene (PIB) and polyethyl acrylate (PEA) could not be performed due
to the unavailability of three experimental contact angle data.

The overall surface tension calculated by Hansen/Beerbower method is satisfactory at first look.
All polymer’s surface free energy are in accordance with the literature. However, the deviation
between the different triplets is very high for some polymers. Especially for PS, when the liquid
triplet consisted of three polar liquids, the overall surface tension fluctuated around 25 mN/m, while
when a non-polar liquid was used, the tension was 20 mN/m higher. For PVC, the use of three polar
liquids led to an overestimation of the overall polymer surface tension. However, for PE and PPS,
the liquid triplet with α-Bromonaphthalene, glycerol, and formamide, did not provide any results,
and three polar liquids were used. Finally, for PVAc and PCL, the dispersion surface components
22
==PAGE:34==
seem to be significantly underestimated from the actual value; however, the total surface free energy
followed the literature.
Although Hansen/Beerbower has used a geometric mean for the hydrogen bonding term and is
usually considered mistaken, the calculated results were adequately comparable with the literature.
6.1
Comparison with van Oss/Good method
One of the significant differences between the two methods is the liquid surface tension compo-
nents. In the van Oss method, these components can be found from reference articles in the litera-
ture. In contrast, the respective terms of the Hansen method are calculated via the Hansen solubility
parameters (HSPs). The LW term of the van Oss method corresponds to the sum of dispersion and
polar terms of Hansen’s approach. In Table 11, the comparison between the solvent surface tension
is presented.

For most of the liquids, the two terms show low divergences. The most distinct difference can be
seen for diiodomethane, where the hydrogen bonding term of the Hansen/Beerbower method equals
17.76 mN/m, while in the van Oss/Good method, it is negligible. Furthermore, the Hansen method
has a hydrogen bonding component with the α-Bromonaphthalene, a non-polar liquid. Finally, the
sum of the dispersion and polar terms of Hansen’s approach for glycerol and ethylene glycol seems
to be underestimated compared to the respective term of van Oss.

7.1
First alternative for Panayiotou calculations
When the Vcosmo/Acosmo and dispersion PSP (σw) of a polymer are known, the dispersion com-
ponent of the solid can be easily calculated from equation (3.14). However, for polymers, the
Vcosmo/Acosmo is hard to be obtained directly, and the monomer approximation should be used.
Nevertheless, for limited polymers, Panayiotou et al. [6] has used the monomer method with two
different monomers for each polymer and evaluated their dispersion surface tension component.
The reported monomers and the polymer’s surface tension are tabulated in Table 15.

The polarizability component can be calculated from one experimental contact angle data with a
polar or non-polar solvent when the dispersion term is known. In this first approach, a non-polar
liquid will be used. Diiodomethane and α-Bromonaphthalene will be the non-polar solvents used
in the following Chapters.

7.1.2
Analog Oligomer approach
Panayiotou et al. [6], have calculated the dispersion component only for the limited polymers
presented in Table 15. The Vcosmo/Acosmo ratio has been reported for many monomers, and it
fluctuates from 0.082 to 0.107. An average value of 0.096 is assumed to be adequate for calculating
the polymer’s ratio, where the available data from monomers is limited. Many different parameters
should be known to evaluate this term for more polymers. These are enumerated as follows:
1. The monomer of each polymer
2. The q and r parameters of the polymer
3. The q and r parameters of the monomer
4. The dispersion partial solvation parameter of the polymer σw
5. The Vcosmo/Acosmo of the monomer that is assumed equal to 0.096

For calculating the polarizability surface tension component, the contact angle of a non-polar liquid
is used. Diiodomethane is the greatest choice as a non-polar liquid because it has been used in
plenty of literature research, so the accuracy of the calculated tension will be high. A non-polar
liquid is preferred over a polar one for these calculations due to negative hydrogen-bonding parts,
which reduces the terms of equation (A.183) and improves, even more, the accuracy of the result.

The overall surface tension of the polymer is the sum of the dispersion, the polarizability, and the
hydrogen-bonding compounds. In the case of PCL, where the hydrogen-bonding is zero, the total
surface tension is the sum of the dispersion and polarizability components.
γs = γw +γpz = 32.93+11.78 ⇒
γs = 44.71 mN/m
PBMA, PEA, and PIB are not hydrogen-bonding polymers like PCL, while Nylon-6 is a polar
polymer, and consequently, an acidic and a basic term should be calculated. The calculation for
their surface tension components can be seen in Appendix A.4.6. A summary of the calculated
surface tensions is provided in Table 20.

Panayiotou et al. [8] have reported a surface tension value of 34.9 mN/m for PBMA, which agrees
with the 35.35 mN/m calculated in this section. The deviation between these values is slightly
higher than 1 %. Wu et al. [26] calculated the surface tension of PBMA equal to 31.2 mN/m by
using the Fowkes equation back in 1971. Of course, a higher deviation was expected using the
Fowkes equation than the better developed Panayiotou equation; nevertheless, the values are very
close.
Biresaw et al. [30] have reported the surface tension of PCL to be 45.5 mN/m by using the Van
Oss/Good method. The deviation between the referenced and the calculated value does not exceed
2%. In the calculation with the van Oss method, the acidic and basic part contributes to the total
surface tension; in contrast with the Panayiotou method that almost in all the polymers, at least one
is equal to zero. In the Panayiotou method, the sum of dispersion and polarizability components
corresponds to the Lewis/van den Waal component of the van Oss/Good equation [6]. Biresaw et
al. [30] report the van der Waals component of PCL to be 45.2 mN/m, which is much closer to the
calculated value of PCL.
Van Oss et al. [50, 51], reported a total surface tension of PIB equal to 25.0 mN/m, which deviates
20% from the calculated value. However, in both cases, the acidic and basic components are 0,
which indicates a non-polar polymer. That can also be seen from the overall surface tension of PIB,
which is much lower than other polymers.
Zdziennicka et al. [34] have calculated the surface tension of Nylon-6 by using Neumann, Owens/
Wendt, and van Oss/Good method. Neumann’s approach was repeated three times with three differ-
ent liquids. The surface tension of Nylon-6 was calculated to be 41.2, 39.5, and 37.90 mN/m for wa-
ter, formamide, and diiodomethane, respectively. Owens/Wendt method was used two times with
two different pairs of liquids. When diiodomethane and formamide were the liquid pair, Nylon-6’s
surface tension fluctuated between 40-41 mN/m. However, when water and diiodomethane were
used, the surface tension fluctuated from 43 to 45 mN/m. The fluctuation in two different cases of
Owens/Wendt happens because of the repeat of the calculations for all the possible range of contact
angles of a liquid. For example, the contact angle of diiodomethane on Nylon-6 fluctuates from
41 to 46 o. Zdziennicka tried all these possible combinations to provide the final surface tension
range. Finally, they calculated the surface tension using the van Oss/Good method with water-
formamide-diiodomethane, equal to 38-40 mN/m. The lowest divergence between the evaluated
surface tension and literature was observed for the case where they used Owens/Wendt method and
formamide-diiodomethane as a liquid pair.
Schwach et al. [32] have reported the surface tension of PEA equal to 59 mN/m which, deviates a
lot from the calculated 42.80 mN/m. However, polymers are low energy surfaces, and their surface
29
==PAGE:41==
tensions are rare to exceed 50 mN/m, so the reported value possibly does not represent the actual
value of this polymer.
Slight deviations can be observed in that method from the assumption for the Vcosmo/Acosmo of the
monomers that were done in equation (3.15). That can explain the deviation of PIB, PEA and
Nylon-6 from the values reported in the literature.

7.2
Second approach-Surface tension calculation with one polar and one

The second alternative of the Panayiotou method is when the ratioVcosmo/Acosmo is unknown, which
means that γw cannot be calculated directly. That leads to a 2x2 equation system, with two exper-
imental data from contact angles and two liquids (one polar and one apolar). The acid and base
components of the liquid are replaced with equations (3.18), and (3.19), and can be calculated after
from γpz, and the partial solvation parameters. For the polymer’s surface tension calculation, three
different pairs of solvents were used. Diiodomethane was tried with water and formamide, while
water was also assessed with α-Bromonaphthalene.
PMMA is the first investigated polymer. PMMA is the first investigated polymer. The detailed
calculations with water and diiodomethane as polar and non-polar solvents liquids can be seen in
equations (7.8) and (7.9). The contact angle of water on PMMA is 80 o, while of diiodomethane
is 41 o [6, 23]. Water- α-Bromonaphthalene and formamide-diiodomethane calculations can be
found in Appendix A.4.7.

7.3
Third approach-Surface tension calculation with two non-polar liquids
A limitation of the Panayiotou method appears when there is no available information for the UNI-
QUAC/UNIFAC parameters that were used in equation (3.15) or the partial solvation parameters
of the polymer. Panayiotou et al. [6] suggest using the contact angle of two non-polar liquids and
solving a system of two equations with two unknowns. The unknown parameters are the solid’s
dispersion and polarizability surface tension components. The partial solvation parameters and
the polarizability component should calculate the acidic or basic surface tension. However, in the
majority of the reported polymers from Panayiotou et al. [7], at least one of the acidic or basic
partial solvation parameters (σGa,σGb) is zero, which means that consequently, at least one of the
acidic or basic surface tension component is zero. For the hydrogen-bonding term, the product of
γa and γb is used. When at least one of these terms is zero, the hydrogen-bonding part is zero.
So, it is a reasonable assumption to calculate the polymer surface tension only from dispersion and
polarizability components.
The surface tension components of some non-polar liquids are provided in Table 28.

calculated at 15.81 and 2.05 mN/m, respectively. The summary calculations for Teflon FEP are

Panayiotou et al. [8] have calculated the surface tension of PTFE equal to 23.7 mN/m by using
the procedure that was done in Chapter 7.1.2 the van der Waal and polarizability components were
reported to be 21.9 and 1.9 mN/m, respectively. That calculation deviates approximately 7% from
the surface tension calculated from two non-polar liquids. The dispersion part is almost equal
between the two methods. The most crucial information about the reported values is the zero acidic
and basic surface tension components. That means that both the acidic and basic partial solvation
is zero, and the assumption used in the beginning was correct. Zdziennicka et al. [34] calculated
the surface tension of PTFE by using the Owens/Wendt method with two different pairs of liquids.
For the water-diiodomethane pair, the calculated tension was 20.39 mN/m, while for formamide-
diiodomethane, it was 20.29 mN/m. These values are also in good agreement with the calculated
value that is presented in Table 31. El Ghzaoui et al. [28] also used the Owens/Wendt method to
show that PTFE’s tension is 19.60 mN/m. In more recent research, Ozbay et al. [22] used both
Owens/Wendt and van Oss/Good equations. Using Owens/Wendt’s theory, the total surface tension
was 14.84 mN/m, with 13.60 and 1.23 mN/m the dispersion and polar components, respectively.
The van Oss/Good theory calculation showed slightly lower tension (14.72 mN/m) with 13.6 mN/m
for the van der Waal component and 1.11 mN/m for the acid-base part. Lee et al. [9] calculated the
polymer’s tension by using the van Oss/Good equation with two different triplets of liquids. With
the triplet diiodomethane-water-glycerol, PTFE’s surface tension was calculated to be 15.51 mN/m,
where van der Waal and acid/base components were 15.37 and 0.14 mN/m, respectively. When α-
Bromonaphthalene was used instead of diiodomethane, the surface tension of PTFE increased to
17.59 mN/m; a value that corresponded entirely to the van der Waal part. Panayiotou method
provides the highest surface tensions for PTFE, while van Oss/Good has the lowest. The tensions
calculated from the van Oss/Good method deviate around 31% from the values from Panayiotou
and around 25% from the computed values of the Owens/Wendt method. Here a critical surface
tension investigation will be helpful to compare with Panayiotou and van Oss’s approach. The
critical surface tension is numerically close to the surface tension of the solid [2]. Zisman et al.
[36] reported that the critical surface tension of PTFE fluctuates from 17.5 to 20.5 mN/m. That is
closer to the values calculated from the Owens/Wendt method, while it deviates a little from the
values calculated from Panayiotou and van Oss/Good methods.
Wu et al. [10] used six different triplets to calculate the surface tension of Teflon FEP with van
Oss/Good theory, while Lee et al. [9] used two other triplets. Their calculations can be seen in
Table 32. The calculated value by using two non-polar liquids and the Panayiotou approach pro-
vided a surface tension equal to 17.82 mN/m. It is in good agreement with the values reported
from Wu et al. [10], where FEP surface tension fluctuated from 15.5 to 15.8 mN/m. Further-
more, a deviation of 3% is observed from the value reported by Lee et al. [9], when the liquid
triplet was α-Bromonaphthalene-water-glycerol. This polymer’s overall low surface tension and
the comparison with literature results confirm the initial assumption that Teflon FEP does not have
hydrogen-bonding surface tension.

According to Wu et al. [10], the surface tension of PET fluctuates from 43.4 to 44.3 mN/m. The
calculated value was 43.24 mN/m using the Panayiotou method with two non-polar liquids, which
is in agreement with the literature. The deviation between these values does not exceed 2%, which
means that the zero hydrogen-bonding compound assumption is correct. The LW part of the van der
Waal method corresponds to the sum of dispersion and polarizability component of the Panayiotou
method [6]. The LW part from Table 33 deviates less than 1% from the calculated surface tension
40
==PAGE:52==
with the Panayiotou approach. Ozbay et al. [22] have calculated the surface tension of PET by
using both van Oss/Good theory and Owens/Wendt method. The van Oss/Good approach used
water-formamide-diiodomethane as a liquid triplet. The surface tension of PET was equal to 40.60
mN/m, which corresponds entirely to van der Waal forces. That result is also in good agreement
with the calculated value of the Panayiotou method, which deviates only 6%. Finally, Ozbay et al.
[22] repeated the calculation by using Owens/Wendt method, where the overall PET tension was
calculated to be 43.59 mN/m, with 40.60 and 2.99 mN/m, the dispersion and polar compounds,
respectively.
Lee et al. [9] used the van Oss method to calculate the surface tension of PVDF. They used water
and glycerol as polar liquids while they tried diiodomethane and α-Bromonaphthalene as non-polar.
When the non-polar liquid was α-Bromonaphthalene, the overall surface tension of PVDF was
32.74 mN/m with 32.70 and 0.04 mN/m for van der Waal and acid-base components, respectively.
When diiodomethane was used instead of α-Bromonaphthalene, the total surface free energy was
30.34 mN/m with 28.58 and 1.76 mN/m for the van der Waal and acid-base tensions, respectively.
The surface tension calculated with the Panayiotou method with two non-polar liquids was 26.72
mN/m. That value was lower than both calculated tensions reported in Lee et al. [9] with the
deviation to be between 12 and 20%. Ozbay et al. [22] were also calculated PVDF’s surface
tension with the van Oss/Good method by using water-formamide and diiodomethane as a liquid
triplet. They estimated the total surface tension to be 32.43 mN/m with the van der Waal part 26.85
mN/m and the acid-base part equal to 5.58 mN/m. Similarly, with the reported values provided by
Lee et al. [9], a high deviation of approximately 20% is observed from the result calculated with
the Panayiotou approach. Finally, Ozbay et al. [22] have used the Owens/Wendt method to repeat
the calculation where they calculated PVDF’s free energy equal to 32.70 mN/m with dispersion
and polar components equivalent to 26.85 and 5.58 mN/m, respectively.
Ozbay et al. [22] calculated PPS’s surface tension by using the van Oss/Good method to be 41.87
mN/m. The van der Waal and acidic and base components were 41.86 and 0.02 mN/m. The
calculated value from the Panayiotou method by using two non-polar liquids was 41.75 mN/m.
These results are in excellent agreement, where they deviate much less than 1%. That indicates
that the initial assumption of zero hydrogen-bonding surface tension is also probably valid for this
polymer.
For comparison reasons the same precedure were repeated also, for the ivenstigated polymers of
the previous Chapters. The polymer’s surface free energy is provided in Table 34. The subscripts
DIM and Br correspond to diiodomethane and α-Bromonaphthalene, respectively.

The method that uses two non-polar liquids seems to work perfectly for some polymers; however,
erroneous contact angles were observed for others. Excellent agreement with the literature and the
previously mentioned methods is observed for PMMA, PVC, PS, PIB, and Nylon-6. In contrast,
that approach shows incompetence in operating for PE, PP, and PBMA. The limited available data
for two or more non-polar liquids hinders the wide use of that alternative. Nevertheless, when there
is much available data, it is suggested to try many different pairs of non-polar liquids and observe
which provides the best solution.
7.4
Comparison of approaches
Table 35: Comparison between the four approaches for the Panayiotou method.
Panayiotou alternative

In Table 35, all the alternatives of the Panayiotou method are summarized. The abbreviation N.P,
corresponds to non-polar liquids, while P. corresponds to polar. The Table’s middle columns refer
to each approach’s known and unknown parameters, while the last column refers to each method’s
accuracy. The most accurate alternative is the first one when a non-polar liquid is used. In that
approach, the dispersion component of the Panayiotou method is known, which reduces the un-
knowns to one. That means that the accuracy of this approach is strongly linked to the accuracy
of the contact angle used. Two non-polar liquids were tried separately with that alternative, and
the results were equally satisfactory. However, when this approach is used with a polar liquid,
the accuracy is not always high because more complex equations can easily create high deviations
from reality. Of the three polar liquids used with that alternative, formamide shows the lowest
divergences with the literature. The same problem was also observed in the second alternative. In
the second approach, many different pairs of polar and non-polar solvents were tried. No solution
existed for a remarkable number of pairs, while high deviations were observed for many polymers.
42
==PAGE:54==
The last approach focused on the calculation of the surface tension components with the use of two
non-polar liquids. That approach shows excellent agreement with the literature for some polymers;
however high divergences for the remaining. It is suggested the method to be used with various
pairs of non-polar liquids to increase its accuracy.

Table 36 shows the comparison between the liquid surface tension components of the Hansen/
Beerbower and Panayiotou methods. The terms of the Hansen/Beerbower method were calculated
in Chapter 6, and they are derived from Hansen solubility parameters (HSPs). In contrast, the
terms of the Panayiotou method were reported in Panayiotou et al. [6], and they are derived from
partial solvation parameters (PSPs). The most significant differences are observed for the non-
polar liquids. For example, diiodomethane has a hydrogen-bonding term in the Hansen/Beerbower
method, while in Panayiotou, this term equals zero. For α-Bromonaphthalene, the polarizability
term of Panayiotou is nine times higher than the polar compound of the Hansen/Beerbower method.
A similar divergence between the polarizability and polar term of the two methods was observed
for glycerol too, where the component of Panayiotou was much higher than Hansen/Beerbower’s
component.
8
Contact angle prediction
The surface tensions can be used to estimate the contact angles of the polymers with a variety of
liquids. These assessed values can be compared with contact angles that have been reported in the
literature. Contact angle prediction can be used as an indicator for the accuracy of the calculated
surface tension. In the previous Chapters the Owens/Wendt, van Oss/Good, Hansen/Beerbower,
and Panayiotou methods were used for surface free energy investigation.
43
==PAGE:55==

The differences between the reported values for formamide, ethylene glycol and water are observed
due to different cleaning methods of the polymer surface. Contact angles are very sensitive to
contamination, so even a tiny amount of dirt on the polymer’s surface can lead to high deviations
between the reported values of contact angles [2].

The term "Spreads" is used here when the cosθ is higher than 1, a non-acceptable value. That
means that the contact angle has a value around 0 o, which is interpreted as an excellent wetting of
the solid surface by that liquid [2]. For formamide, the predicted value was 37.62 o, while the value
reported in Rosa et al. [25] was 57.70 o. The deviation between the two values is higher than 50 %.
That can probably happen if at least one of the used contact angles for surface tension calculation
was not accurate enough.

Zdziennicka et al. [34, 35] have reported the contact angles of many solvents on Nylon-6. The

much lower theoretical value, which is in the denominator of the deviation equation.

For almost all the investigated polymers, the liquid triplet used was diiodomethane-water-formamide.
For polystyrene (PS), glycerol was used instead of formamide in the polar liquid pair. Meanwhile,
α-Bromonaphthalene replaced diiodomethane for Teflon FEP’s surface free energy calculation. Fi-
nally, formamide, ethylene glycol, and paraffin oil consisted the liquid triplet used for PVAc. The
contact angle prediction happens when the polymers’ calculated surface tension components are
substituted in the van Oss/Good equation and solved with only an unknown parameter, the contact
angle θ.

Prediction with Hansen/Beerbower method
In Table 91 are presented the polymers used for the contact angle prediction with the Hansen/
Beerbower method. The Table includes the surface tension components of the Hansen method
and the liquid triplet that was used for each calculation. Due to limited contact angle data, the
prediction for polyisobutylene (PIB) and polyethyl acrylate (PEA) could not be performed. That
method requires three liquids with the respective contact angles on the polymer’s surface. However,
the available data for PIB and PEA were only two liquids.

Polyethylene’s predicted contact angles deviations fluctuate from 19 to 32 % from the angles re-
ported in Balkenende et al. [40], with the only exception for diiodomethane, where the divergence
between literature and estimation was 11.5 %. An explanation for these significant deviations is the
use of three polar liquids, for the surface tension component calculation, instead of two polar and
one non-polar. Furthermore, the symmetric hydrogen-bonding term in Hansen/Beerbower equation
possibly affects the final result.

The lack of available data hinders the comparison between literature for these two polymers. How-
ever, some conclusions can be deduced from the predicted contact angles. Firstly, the contact angle
of diiodomethane on PVAc’s surface deviates more than 50 % from the angle reported by Ucar et al.
[24]. Secondly, for PEO the contact angle of the non-polar α-Bromonaphthalene is not reasonable
to be higher than the one of ethylene glycol and DMSO. That occurs from the surface tension com-
ponents of that polymer. Despite an excellent overall surface tension, the respective components
seem to be mistaken. The dispersion component is much lower than the hydrogen-bonding one,
which leads to the inaccuracy of contact angle prediction.

Similarly, with the polymers where three polar liquids were used for the surface tension calculation

8.4
Prediction with Panayiotou method
Panayiotou method has many different approaches depending on the available literature data. For
the contact angle prediction, two techniques will be used. The approach where the dispersion sur-
72
==PAGE:84==
face tension is known and the rest of the components are calculated from a non-polar liquid is the
first approach that is used. The best choice of the non-polar liquid is diiodomethane, where the
available database is extensive, which leads to higher accuracy in the calculated surface tension
components. That approach also includes the case where the dispersion component was calcu-
lated from the monomer and the UNIQUAC/UNIFAC parameters. The second approach used for
prediction is the case where the dispersion component is known, and a polar liquid is used to cal-
culate the rest of the equation terms. The chosen polar liquid is water, due to higher availability
in contact angles. Nevertheless, in Chapter 7.1.4, the calculated surface tension was not accurate
enough, so only three polymers will be investigated with that approach. The surface tension com-
ponents of many solvents are presented in Table 107. Unfortunately, the polymers calculated with
two non-polar liquids can not be evaluated for contact angle prediction due to the limited knowl-
edge of its acidic and basic components. The method with the two non-polar liquids has assumed
a zero hydrogen-bonding term; however, the polymers are likely to have at least an acid or base
component.

8.4.1
Prediction with Panayiotou method and Diiodomethane
In Table 38, the contact angles of many different solvents on PMMA have been summarized.
By having the surface components of both polymers and liquids, the only unknown parameter of
the Panayiotou equation is the contact angle θ. PMMA is the first investigated polymer, while
glycerol is the first solvent.

The predicted contact angles agree with most liquids’ literature reported contact angles. Owens
et al. [23] mentioned the contact angle of water on PVC was equal to 87.0 o, while the predicted
value was 92.5 o. That gives a deviation of 5.93% between the literature and predicted values. The
highest divergence observed for formamide was the predicted contact angle, which deviated 19.1
% from the value reported in Volpe et al. [39]. However, Ozbay et al. [22] mentioned a contact
angle of 66 o for formamide on PVC, which deviates less than 6%. An excellent agreement is
observed between the estimated contact angle of α-Bromonaphthalene on PVC’s surface with the
value reported in Volpe et al. [39].

For formamide, water, and ethylene glycol, both Good et al. [3], and Volpe et al. [39] have reported

8.4.2
Prediction with Panayiotou method and Water
In this part, similar calculations with Chapter 8.4.1 are repeated and can be seen in Appendix B.5.
The only difference is that the surface tension components of the polymers have been calculated
with water instead of diiodomethane. Unfortunately, that method is susceptible to small contact
angle divergences from actual values, so consequently, for some polymers, the deviations will
79
==PAGE:91==
be very high. Due to its inadequate accuracy in the surface tension component calculation, only
PMMA, PVC, and PS are used for prediction with that approach.

8.5.2
Parity plots
Parity plots are used to compare experimental and theoretical values. The y-axis consists of the
predicted contact angles, while the x-axis of the literature reported angles. The diagonal line (y=x)
81
==PAGE:93==
corresponds to experimental data measured in the literature. The coherence of one point with the
literature can be seen from its divergence from the diagonal line. The predicted contact angle equals
the referenced contact angle when a point lies on the diagonal line. In that Chapter, parity plots are
created only for the polymers with adequate available data.
Figure 2: (a): Parity plot for PMMA and Owens/Wendt method, (b): Parity plot for PMMA and van
Oss/Good method, (c): Parity plot for PMMA and Hansen/Beerbower method, (d): Parity plot for PMMA
and Panayiotou method-1st approach.
82
==PAGE:94==
Figure 3: Combined parity plots for PMMA.
In Figure 2, the four methods used for PMMA’s surface tension investigation are compared. Van
Oss/Good and Panayiotou’s results are the most coherent with literature methods. Especially, van
Oss points almost lie to the diagonal line. On the other hand, the highest divergences can be seen
with Owens/Wendt method, where all the points are scattered around the line.
Figure 3, shows the combination of the parity plots presented in Figure 2. For the Panayiotou
method, all the points are aside from the diagonal line; however, one point diverges more than the
others. That point corresponds to the predicted angle of DMSO, for which the Panayiotou method
could not predict well its contact angle.
Figure 4 shows the divergence of predicted contact angles, indicated with blue dots, from the ex-
perimental values reported in the literature. The most accurate methods seem to be Panayiotou, van
Oss/Good, and Hansen/Beerbower, where the blue dots almost coincide with the orange diagonal
line. However, Owens/Wendt diverges from the literature for most of the probed liquids. In Figure
5 all the methods are combined into one plot. From that figure, it is distinct that the Owens/Wendt
method could not adequately predict the investigated liquids’ contact angles.
83
==PAGE:95==
Figure 4: (a): Parity plot for PVC and Owens/Wendt method, (b): Parity plot for PVC and van Oss/Good
method, (c): Parity plot for PVC and Hansen/Beerbower method, (d): Parity plot for PVC and Panayiotou
method-1st approach.
Figure 5: Combined parity plots for PVC.

approach.
From Figures 6 and 7, it is distinguished that Panayiotou and van Oss/Good are the best methods
compared to the others for PS. Especially the Panayiotou method has three points lying on the diag-
onal line. On the other hand, Owens/Wendt show high deviations, while for Hansen/Beerbower, the
dots that correspond to predicted contact angles are close to the line that corresponds to literature.

==PAGE:97==
Figure 7: Combined parity plots for PS.

Owens/Wendt’s methods have excellent agreement with the literature for that polymer. Panayiotou’s
86
==PAGE:98==
theoretical data points are very close to the line that corresponds to the experimental data. Nev-
ertheless, for Hansen/Beerbower method, the dots that correspond to predicted contact angles are
scattered around the diagonal line.
Figure 9: Combined parity plots for PE.
The parity plots of PP are shown in Figures 10 and 11. The dots corresponding to predicted contact
angles and the literature significantly coincide with the Panayiotou method. The majority of the
theoretical points lie on the experimental data line. Owens/Wendt and van Oss/Good also have good
correspondence between the two pairs of dots, while in contrast, Hansen/Beerbower method has
the highest divergences among literature and predicted contact angles. Despite having the highest
divergences among the methods, the dots corresponding to Hansen/Beerbower’s predicted values
are not so far from the diagonal line.

Figure 13: Combined parity plots for PBMA.
In Figures 13 and 12, the divergences between predicted and experimental contact angles are pre-
sented graphically. Owens/Wendt and van Oss/Good approaches have many similarities and slight
89
==PAGE:101==
divergences from the diagonal line. In contrast, for the Panayiotou method, most of the dots are on
the line or closely near it. Hansen/Beerbower’s method has three dots in excellent agreement with
literature. However, the remaining two points have remarkable deviations.
Figures 14 and 15 show the comparison of parity plots among the four methods. Van Oss/Good
and Owens/Wendt’s methods are completely inaccurate for the contact angle prediction of PCL.
Hansen/Beerbower has two points in excellent agreement with the diagonal line; however, the point
corresponding to ethylene glycol shows high deviations from the literature. Finally, the Panayiotou
method performs perfectly in the contact angle prediction of that polymer. That excellent agreement
for that method and the weak agreement of the remaining approaches can occur when at least one
contact angle of a liquid pair or triplet is not accurate enough. Panayiotou’s method used only one
experimental data from contact angles, so its accuracy is linked to the accuracy of that angle.

method-1st approach.

==PAGE:103==
Owens/Wendt and Hansen/Beerbower have also a good agreement with the literature. Panayiotou
method is the most inaccurate for that polymer. An explanation for these divergences is the as-
sumption made for the surface tension components calculation in Chapter 7.1.2. Possibly that
assumption affected the accuracy of the result.
Figure 17: Combined parity plots for Nylon-6.
9
Discussion
The calculated surface tension of polymethyl methacrylate (PMMA) and polyvinyl chloride (PVC)
have shown slight deviations between van Oss/Good, Owens/Wendt, Hansen/Beerbower, and Pana-
yiotou equations (Table 125). In contrast, when polystyrene (PS) is calculated with Owens/Wendt
method, it has a divergence of approximately 8 mN/m from the rest of the equations. Compared
with Hansen/Beerbower method, its total surface free energy is higher than 10 mN/m. Polystyrene
is a non-hydrogen-bonding compound, so using a symmetrical geometrical mean rule for specific
interactions was not expected to affect the result. The dispersion component of PS seems to have
good agreement between Owens/Wendt and Panayiotou, as seen in Table 126. The high deviation
comes from the polar interaction, where Owens/Wendt result has a meagre value. The surface
tensions of polyethylene (PE), polypropylene (PP), and polyvinyl acetate (PVAc) are in excellent
agreement between Owens/Wendt, van Oss/Good, and Panayiotou methods, while the result of the
Hansen/Beerbower method is slightly higher. In contrast, the total surface free energy of polyethy-
lene oxide (PEO) is higher with Owens/Wendt method than in the remaining three. The tension
of polybutyl methacrylate (PBMA) fluctuates between 34 and 35 mN/m for Owens/Wendt, van
Oss/Good, and Panayiotou method, while for Hansen/Beerbower equals 38 mN/m. Polyisobuty-
lene’s (PIB) calculated surface tension with the van Oss/Good and Hansen/Beerbower me- thod,
assumes that the polymer is non-polar. Consequently, its acid and basic surface tension compo-
nents are zero. Panayiotou and Hansen/Beerbower’s methods evaluate the surface tension of PIB
to fluctuate between 28 and 31 mN/m, while the remaining methods between 22 and 24 mN/m.
All the methods agree with the calculated result for polyethyl acrylate (PEA) and fluorinated ethy-
92
==PAGE:104==
lene propylene (FEP). Owens/Wendt, and Panayiotou’s approaches have shown the surface tension
of Nylon-6 around 45 mN/m, while the remaining methods show the surface free energy slightly
lower than 40 %. A similar fluctuation was observed in the literature, depending on the liquids used.
Owens/Wendt and van Oss/Good methods are in excellent agreement for polytetrafluoroethylene
(PTFE), polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF), and polyphenylene sulfide (PPS). Hansen/Beerbower and
Panayiotou’s method showed PVDF’s surface tension 5 mN/m higher than the other methods. PPS’s
and PVDF’s surface tensions, calculated by the Hansen/Beerbower method, are overestimated com-
pared to the other methods. The surface free energy of polyethylene terephthalate (PET) has minor
divergences between the van Oss/Good, Hansen/Beerbower, and Panayiotou approaches. Mean-
while, it is 3 mN/m higher when Owens/Wendt method is used.
For most polymers, the LW term of van Oss/Good equals the dispersion term of the Owens/Wendt
method. That happened because diiodomethane was used as a non-polar liquid. When a non-polar
liquid is used, the two equations coincide. Regarding the Panayiotou method, various assumptions
have been used for half of the polymers. For the polymers calculated with the monomer approach,
the assumption of a steady the Vcosmo/Acosmo ratio was used. Furthermore, it was assumed that
the hydrogen-bonding term equals zero for the polymers for which two non-polar liquids were
used. The second hypothesis is reasonable because almost all the investigated polymers have zero
hydrogen-bonding terms. That occurred from the partial solvation parameters of the polymers,
where at least one of the acid and basic PSPs were zero.
Some typical literature reported surface tensions are used in Table 125 to discern the divergences
from the calculated values of the previous Chapters. These values do not represent the actual surface
tensions because they have been estimated from contact angles and have not been assessed directly
from an experiment. Thus, that column is used to get an idea about the fluctuation of the polymer’s
surface free energy. All the literature reported surface tensions have been calculated either with the
Owens/Wendt or the van Oss/Good method. Consequently, the evaluated free energy may coincide
with the reported one for some polymers, where the same liquid pair or triplet was used. However,
that does not mean it is mistaken when the Panayiotou method slightly diverges from the literature
and the remaining approaches like PVAc.
The liquid surface tension components used in these methods differ. The terms from the Panayiotou
method come from the solvents’ partial solvation parameters (PSPs), while the terms from Hansen/
Beerbower are derived from the Hansen solubility parameters (HSPs). In contrast, the compo-
nents used in Owens/Wendt and van Oss/Good methods coincide. The decimals of the overall
liquid surface tension components of the four methods were adjusted to equal the ones reported by
Panayiotou et al. [6] in order to have a fair comparison between the four approaches. For example,
in many literature articles, the surface tension of diiodomethane equals 50.80 mN/m. However,
Panayiotou reported it equal to 49.92 mN/m. These small changes do not affect the final result,
but their significance is high for comparing the methods. In general, Van Oss/Good method was in
excellent agreement with the Panayiotou method for the cases where no assumption was made. At
the same time, slight differences can be observed for the remaining polymers.

In Table 127, are summarized the polymer surface tensions calculated with all the different ap-
proaches of the Panayiotou method. The dispersion surface tension component (γw) is known in
the five first columns. For the seven first polymers, the dispersion component has been evaluated
in the literature by Panayiotou et al. [6], from two different monomers of each polymer. The dis-
persion component of the remaining polymers was calculated in Chapter 7.1.2, with one monomer
and the approximation of constant vcosmo/Acosmo ratio. Afterwards, the polarizability component
was calculated with five different solvents with the dispersion part known. Diiodomethane and α-
Bromonaphthalene were the non-polar liquids used, while water, formamide, and ethylene glycol
were the polar ones.
Furthermore, when there were limited information about at least one term of the monomer method,
one polar and one non-polar liquid were used in a 2x2 system with two unknowns to evaluate
the dispersion and polarizability components. The hydrogen-bonding component was calculated
after that from the interconnecting equations. Finally, when the partial solvation parameters of
the polymer were also unknown, the approach of two equations with two unknowns with two
experimental contact angle data can be used.
The most satisfactory approach was the first when the dispersion surface tension component was
known, and the experimental contact angle data from diiodomethane was used. In general, using a
non-polar liquid provides excellent overall surface tensions; however, diiodomethane is preferred
from α-Bromonaphthalene due to higher contact angle availability in the literature. When a po-
lar liquid is used, agreement with literature is observed for some polymers. Nevertheless, slight
deviations from the literature and the first approaches can be observed. The use of two non-polar
solvents for the total surface tension calculation should be used very carefully. Different pairs of
liquids can provide different results. A similar problem is observed for the approach with one polar
and one non-polar liquid. When there is adequate contact angle data, it is suggested to use many
different pairs of solvents for the surface tension calculation. Then the overall surface free energy
of the polymer will be derived from their average. In the approach with one polar and one non-polar
liquid, the presented results of each polymer are the best solutions among the liquid triplets used in
95
==PAGE:107==
Chapter 7.2. So despite having satisfactory results for most polymers, many liquid pairs provided
entirely mistaken outcomes.
Table 127 does not contain the polymers that were calculated with the approach with the two non-
polar liquids. The remaining approaches require the knowledge of partial solvation parameters and
the dispersion component of the Panayiotou method.
During the calculative part, each method’s limitations were observed. The basic term of the van
Oss/Good method was overestimated for many polymers. Furthermore, the symmetric "spec" and
hydrogen bonging terms of Owens/Wendt and Hansen/Beerbower methods lead to slight diver-
gences in the overall surface tension.
However, were there any limitations of the Panayiotou
method? A difficulty of that method is the very expertise required knowledge that someone should
have to calculate the partial solvation parameters of the polymers. When the polymer PSPs are
unknown, one must know quantum chemistry and statistical thermodynamics to find these parame-
ters. Of course, the method with the two non-polar liquids can be used, but the accuracy fluctuated
depending on the reported contact angles.
